==PAGE:7==
Abstract
Municipal solid waste (MSW) is a good alternative fuel for power plants and
the problem of waste landll can be partially solved by recovering its energy.
Moreover, the space devoted to waste storage could be used for others human
activities. Besides this, Waste to Energy technology allows the reduction of pol-
lution and greenhouse emissions on a global scale by avoiding fossil fuels use.
One of the main advantage of using waste instead of other renewable energy
sources, such as solar and wind energy, is that less problems of availability are
observed.
In the present study waste is introduced in a gasication process and a synthe-
sis gas (syngas) is obtained. The gas composition is strongly related to waste
and reactor features. The syngas is suitable for fuelling Solid Oxide Fuel Cell
(SOFC) plant after the removal of impurities, in particular hydrogen sulde is
really dangerous for the cells.
The integration of gasication and SOFC plants with Stirling engine, gas turbine
and steam cycle is investigated in the literature. The combination of integrated
gasication SOFC plant with a steam cycle has been chosen for the development
of the trigeneration plant under analysis in this study.
In addition, the recovery of o-gas energy after the heat recovery steam gener-
ator could be fullled by feeding an adsorption chiller.
Six dierent plant designs are investigated in this study, three congurations
consider the use of an anode pre-heater (AP) while the others show a methana-
tor instead of the heat exchanger. The size of the solutions investigated is in
the range between 20 and 33 MW. A detailed description is provided of the
three dierent examined solutions, with AP and methanator respectively. Sim-
ulations were run with DNA (Dynamic Network Analysis), a component-based
simulation tool for energy systems analysis developed at the Mechanical Engi-
neering department at Technical University of Denmark. The feasibility of a
==PAGE:8==
iv
trigeneration plant for the electricity, heating and cooling production is tested.
After having analysed the eect of hybrid recuperator introduction, the focus
is on the design with split amount of cell stacks in two blocks. This last con-
guration is developed for both anode pre-heater and methanator design; good
plant eciency is shown in both cases by reaching values around 57% and 58%
respectively.
An economic evaluation of all the studied plants is carried out. Results obtained
for two stack blocks designs are promising also from an economic point of view.
==PAGE:9==
Sommario
Il riuto solido urbano è una buona alternativa ai combustibili tradizionali per
gli impianti di potenza ed il problema dello stocaggio dei riuti in discarica può
essere parzialmente risolto grazie al recupero del loro contenuto energetico. In-
oltre, lo spazio dedicato allo stoccaggio dei riuti potrebbe essere utilizzato per
altre attività. Oltre a ciò, grazie all'evitato utilizzo di combustibili fossili, la
tecnologia Waste to Energy consente la riduzione di inquinamento ed emissioni
di gas serra. Uno dei vantaggi principali dell'uso di riuti solidi urbani rispetto
ad altre fonti rinnovabili, come ad esempio l'energia solare ed eolica, è che sono
presenti meno problemi di disponibilità.
In questo studio il riuto è introdotto in un processo di gassicazione ed il gas di
sintesi (syngas) è così ottenuto. La composizione del gas è fortemente collegata
alle caratteristiche del riuto e del reattore. Dopo la rimozione delle impurità,
il syngas è adatto per alimentare un impianto di Solid Oxide Fuel Cell (SOFC),
in particolare l'acido soldrico è dannoso per le celle.
L'intgrazione di impianti SOFC con motori Stirling, turbogas e gruppo a vapore
è investigata in letteratura. L'integrazione di un impianto SOFC con un gruppo
a vapore è stata scelta per lo sviluppo dell'impianto di trigenerazione analizzato
in questo studio.
Inoltre, il recupero dell'energia degli o-gas dopo l'heat recovery steam genera-
tor può essere realizzata alimentando un ciclo frigorifero ad assorbimento.
Sei diversi designs di impianto sono investigati in questo studio, tre cong-
urazioni considerano l'utilizzo di un anode pre-heater (AP) mentre le altre
mostrano un metanatore al posto dello scambiatore di calore. La taglia delle
soluzioni investigate è compresa nel range fra 20 e 33 MW. Una descrizione det-
tagliata è fornita delle tre diverse soluzioni esaminate, rispettivamente con AP
e con metanatore. Le simulazioni sono state eseguite con DNA (Dynamic Net-
work Analysis), un software per la simulazione di sistemi energetici sviluppato
==PAGE:10==
vi
presso il dipartimento di Ingegneria Meccanica della Danmaks Tekniske Unier-
sitet (DTU). La fattibiltà di un impianto di trigenerazione per la produzione di
elettricità, riscaldamento e rarescamento è testata.
Dopo aver analizzato l'eetto dell'introduzione dell'hybrid recuperator, l'attenzione
è focalizzata sul design che prevede la ripartizione delle celle in due blocchi.
Quest'ultima congurazione è sviluppata sia per il design con anode pre-heater
che per quello con metanatore; una buona ecienza di impianto è ottenuta in
entrambi i casi, 57% e 58% rispettivamente.
Una analisi economica di ogni impianto studiato è eseguita. I risultati ottenuti
per i designs con due blocchi di celle separati mostrano risultati promettenti
anche da un punto di vista economico.

Chapter 1
Introduction
The aim of the project is to investigate power plant designs for trigeneration
of electricity, heating and cooling by gasifying municipal solid waste. Dierent
congurations are developed and thermodynamically studied. Furthermore, an
economic evaluation is given.
1.1
Problem Statement
The production of waste is strictly connected with the consumption. Recent
studies expect a constant increase of waste production over the coming years.
Both quantity and composition of waste are aected from socioeconomic factors
like urbanization, industrialization, income level and also from the climate. The
most performing waste management solutions integrate all the given alterna-
tives to reduce the production and minimize the landll.
Waste has an energy content and Waste to Energy is the technology that allows
its recovery. Moreover, the use of waste for electricity and heating production
reduces the problem of waste disposal and treatment. Waste to Energy tech-
nology decreases the dependence on fossil fuels and increases the production
of energy from renewable sources. the average waste consists of 56% biogenic
and 44% non-biogenic materials [1]. The combustion of the biogenic fraction of
MSW is considered renewable by the Unite States Department of Energy [2].
==PAGE:26==
2
Introduction
Also the European Union evaluates as renewable energy the part of energy pro-
duced by organic waste, that is the biodegradable part. In this regard it has to
be reminded the 2020 package of the European Commission, it is a regulation
to ensure the achievement of climate and energy goals for the year 2020. The
three targets are [3]
 20% cut in greenhouse gas emissions compared with 1990,
 20% of EU energy demand from renewable sources,
 20% improvement in energy eciency.
In order to maximize the eciency, new solutions have to be studied. Gasi-
cation enters the market recently, this technology shows promising prospects.
The synthesis gas produced through gasication process is suitable to feed solid
oxide fuel cells (SOFC), with this fuel cells should be possible to achieve high
eciency. In addition, the recovery of the energy content of the o-gas is pos-
sible. Fuel cells o-gas and o-air can be combusted into a burner, ue gas
can than be used to feed a Stirling engine, a gas turbine or a steam cycle. In
addition to the electricity production it is also possible to produce hot water for
district heating. Further recovery of ue gas energy can be performed by the
absorption chiller technology. Two are the advantages obtainable: reduction of
the wasted energy and cooling power production.
Trigeneration is the simultaneous production of electricity, heating and cool-
ing. Signicant reduction of greenhouse emissions can be obtained with this
technology.
1.2
Object of the Project
The scope of this project is to carry out thermodynamic and economic analysis
of trigeneration power plants.
In order to reduce problems concerning materials corrosion and costs, the re-
search on solid oxide fuel cells aims at decreasing of their working temperature.
This reduction would make the integration of SOFC plant with steam cycle
more suitable.
The trigeneration plants in this study run on municipal solid waste.
Gasi-
cation, SOFC, steam cycle and absorption chiller technologies are integrated
together to achieve the goal. Thermodynamic simulations are run with DNA
(Dynamic Network Analysis), a component-based simulation tool for energy
systems analysis developed at the Mechanical Engineering department, Ther-
mal Energy Systems section, at Technical University of Denmark.
==PAGE:27==
1.3 Content of Thesis
3
Six designs are developed and investigated.
The most performing solutions,
considering similar operating conditions for all of them, want to be found. The
optimization of all the congurations is carried out and the most promising de-
sign wants to be discovered. All the plants are economically evaluated to see
which is the most feasible.
1.3
Content of Thesis
Document chapters are outlined below.
1.3.1
Chapter 2: Municipal Solid Waste
The global scenario of municipal solid waste is introduced. Particular atten-
tion is paid to waste composition. The set of the problems concerning waste
treatment is analysed. Waste to Energy technology is introduced with specic
attention to air pollution. The comparison between combustion and gasication
technology is described.
1.3.2
Chapter 3: Technology Overview
The main technologies employed for the development of the trigeneration plant
are introduced in this chapter. Gasication and Solid Oxide Fuel Cell are out-
lined and studies about their integration with Stirling engine, gas turbine and
steam cycle are shown. District heating and absorption plant technology are
also mentioned in the chapter.
1.3.3
Chapter 4: Methodology
DNA models for gasier and SOFC are explained in detail. The project devel-
opment is described by introducing the methods used for the implementation
and optimization of the plants. The model realised in Microsoft Excel©, for
economic analysis and net present value evaluation, is introduced in this chapter.
==PAGE:28==
4
Introduction
1.3.4
Chapter 5: Development of Plant Models
Six dierent congurations of the plant are illustrated. Each design shows some
dierences; trigeneration plant without hybrid recuperator, with the hybrid re-
cuperator and with the split amount of fuel cell stacks. All the solutions are
separately analysed with both anode pre-heater and methanator. DNA results
for each plant are shown and compared in an attempt to identify the most
promising one.
1.3.5
Chapter 6: Model Optimization
Optimization in terms of thermal eciency of the plant is carried out. The
inuence of the solid oxide fuel cell parameters is analysed. In particular fuel
utilization factor, operating temperature and the number of stacks are studied.
Simulations were run and the results are shown in the graphics of this chapter.
The outcome is compared and analysed to nd the best and most promising
solution.
1.3.6
Chapter 7: Economic Analysis
The last chapter is dedicated to the results obtained by the model implemented
for the economic analysis. All the solutions investigated during the study, opti-
mized and not, are economically evaluated. Results are shown and compared.
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 2
Municipal Solid Waste
The production of municipal solid waste has increased by 10 times in the last
century. One of the main causes of this growth is the increment of urbanization
level. Hoornweg et al. show in [4] and in [5] as in 2012 it was expected that
the production of waste would be doubled again by 2015. To give an idea of
how much waste is produced, it is possible to refer to the average United State
citizen who throws away about his weight in the form of garbage in a month.
The problem of waste production is becoming a highly topical issue all over
the world. In particular, China and Brazil have been subjected to a really fast
industrialization process that unavoidably leads to the necessity of good waste
management solutions development.
2.1
Municipal Solid Waste
The waste production is strongly connected with the auence that is strictly
related to the income level and also to the quality of life. In [4] is supposed that
extending the actual socioeconomic trends up 2100 the waste production peak
will not be reached in this century. Figure 2.1 shows the past and predicted
trend of waste production calculated with three dierent methods.
On the base of past data and by considering the same level of auence, it is
possible to see that the generation of waste in the countries is two times less
than in the cities [5]. In 1900 the 13% of the population (about 220 million of
people) living in the cities produced less than 300000 tonnes of rubbish per day.
==PAGE:30==
6
Municipal Solid Waste
Figure 2.1: Past and future trend of MSW production analysed with three
dierent methods. The rst shared socioeconomic pathway [6][5],
represented by the gray line, supposes that the 90% of the 7 billion
population will be urbanized and more environmentally conscious.
The blue curve supposes a population of 9.5 billion of inhabitants
and a level of urbanization of 80%. Finally the red line presumes
13.5 billion of people on the planet and an urbanization of 70%.
In 2000 the 49% of the population was urbanized (about 2.9 billion of people)
and generated 3 million tonnes of waste per day.
The higher production of waste takes along the problem of the waste manage-
ment. Actually storing waste in the landll is not the best solution to think
of. The storage requires big area that would be no more usable for any other
purpose, furthermore the emission of chemical substances would increase the
pollution. Although new technologies under study try to nd better solutions
to improve the environmental situation this is still an important problem. Many
countries have developed dierent methods to recover waste. Actually one of
the most employed technology consist of incinerate the waste to produce energy,
better known as Waste to Energy technology.
Municipal Solid Waste Composition
Dierent studies are carried out to describe the composition of municipal solid
waste. Nevertheless, there are many variables that aected the composition of
waste like season, region and income level.
Data about type of waste in four dierent countries are shown in Table 2.1

In the study conducted by Milke et al. [11], the variability of MSW composition
with a log-logistic distribution is shown. The study is based on the tting of
twenty-two detailed analyses of solid waste from British Columbia, Canada,
using best t software. It is proved that the log-logistic distribution ts a wide
variety of waste composition.
Ozbay and Durmusoglu in [12] show moisture content data related to the income
level.
They say that low-income areas produce waste with higher moisture
content (60-75%) due to the higher production of organic garbage, high-income
areas produce less organic waste, meaning that the moisture content is lower
(20-30%). This is the reason why the range of the average energy content is
wide, usually the value is included between 9 and 20 MJ/kg (Franjo et al., 1992;
World Bank, 1999).
The generation of waste produced by a family in a week is investigated in [13],
three dierent types of family are under study: nuclear, extended and mono-
parental. The nuclear family is composed by a head of the household, the spouse
(if there is one) and an unmarried children. The extended family includes two
or more family members by direct line and of the same sex and their families,
all of them share a house and are under the same authority of the head of the
house. The last category is the composed family which is made by a nuclear or
extended family but the people do not have any tie of kinship with the head of
the family. The sample of the study was of 125 families: 67 nuclear families, 45
extended families and 13 mono-parental families. Figure 2.2 shows the waste
composition on the basis of family topology.
Ojeda-Benítez et al. [13] focus also the production rates by family topology, day
of the week and socioeconomic level. Figure 2.3 shows the results.
From the previous data it is possible to see as the stream of waste generation
varies between week-days and weekend. Moreover, it is believed that the changes
in the stream composition could be due to dierent city-behavioural patterns.
==PAGE:32==
8
Municipal Solid Waste
Figure 2.2: Residential Solid Waste Composition by Family Topology taken
from [13].
Figure 2.3 presents data that highlight as, for each family topology, the medium
income level produces much waste than low income level and high income level.
In the paper [14] Rokni summarizes dierent waste compositions studied in the
literature. Table 2.2 shows these results.
Data of this paragraph show that the variability of municipal solid waste depends
on a lot of dierent variables. The system adopted to treat the waste has to be
optimized on the basis of the waste composition, this factor strongly inuence
the performance of the system itself.
Since the composition of MSW is extremely variable also the caloric value of
waste can change a lot. The introduction of Waste to Energy (WTE) shows that,
on the basis of a chemical analysis, the MSW average composition can be stated
by the formula C6H10O4 [17]. The reaction occurring during the combustion of
this compound is:
C6H10O4 + 6.5O2 →6CO2 + 5H2O + 23.5MJ/kg

2.1 Municipal Solid Waste

2.1.1
Municipal Solid Waste in Europe and Denmark
The European Commission stated basic notions and denitions related to waste
managements in Europe. The Directive 2008/98/CE summarises some basic
rules for waste management: waste have to be managed without carrying any
risk for human health and environment. The basic concept for the management
of waste can be schematized in the pyramid of Figure 2.4.
Figure 2.4: Waste Management Hierarchy. Inspired to [18].
The rst provision is to take measures to limit waste production, through the
waste treatment during the design process of a product and the use of less
harmful products it is possible to approach the goal.
In order to reach the
==PAGE:36==
12
Municipal Solid Waste
minimization of waste production, the second measure given by the directive of
the European Commission is to re-use the waste. When the re-use is not possible
it should be used for a dierent scope, this is the recycling of waste. In case that
no re-use and no recycle are possible the waste can be recovered, for example it
can be used for the production of energy. The last ranking solution is the storage
in landll, this warehousing can provoke atmospheric pollution and also ground
and water contamination. The goal is to reach zero waste storage in landll. In
Denmark landll of suitable waste for incineration has been forbidden.
Successful MSW management expects all the options shown in the hierarchy in
varying levels. The setting of performing only the most performable option is
unrealistic, it can lead to similar results obtained by performing only the least
performable option. The achievement of sustainable MSW management is a
target for all countries. It has to be highlighted as higher recycling rates are
achieved in communities employing integrated management systems, probably
it is due to a greater awareness.
The production of waste in Denmark from 2001 to 2010, based on Eurostat data
of 2012, is shown in Figure 2.5. It is possible to observe a slow increasing in
waste generation before 2008 and a larger decline of generation (around 12%)
between 2008 and 2010, this is especially due to the change in regulation from
1 January 2010.
Figure 2.5: MSW per capita production in Denmark. Taken from [19].
Denmark, unlike other European countries, decided to recover the energy of
the waste through the incineration. Three main aspects must be highlighted
regarding waste management in Denmark [19]
 Only 4% of the waste in Denmark is stored in landll while 45% is incin-
==PAGE:37==
2.2 From Waste to Energy
13
erated (2010 data).
 In 2009 Denmark was recycling around 50% of waste. This percentage
decreased in 2010 while changing the regulation on recyclable waste. The
goal is now to achieve 50% recycling level, based on 2010 data, by 2020.
 Initiatives to better manage MSW in Denmark were taken before 2001 (e.g.
landll ban, landll and incineration tax, separate collection schemes).
2.2
From Waste to Energy
The European Union evaluates as a renewable energy source the organic part of
municipal solid waste. What distinguishes waste from others renewable energy
sources, like for example wind energy, is that it is less vulnerable to variability.
In this section, as wall as in the rest on the study, Waste to Energy means the
conversion of MSW into electric power, without considering medical, hazardous
and other types of waste. Actually the most common way to convert waste to
energy is the combustion, many other alternative treatments are continuously
under study.
As already mentioned in the previous paragraph, the extraction of energy from
waste is more advisable than storing waste in landll. It can be observed a big
dierence in electricity production between waste to energy plants and landll
provided with landll-gas capture and energy generation, this dierence is more
than 500 kWh for ton of waste [20].
In Europe Waste to Energy technology is attractive because it leads to a reduc-
tion of greenhouse gas emissions. Greenhouse gases are those gases that have an
impact on climate changes. Moreover, the high fees charged by European Union
on waste landlling have realized a favourable environment for the development
of this technology.
2.2.1
Air Pollution
During the development of a Waste to Energy plant particular attention has to
be paid to emissions control, this research eld is continually under investigation.
Limits on pollution emissions depend on the local legislations but common rules
have to be followed [21]:
 Limitation of pollutants emission.
 Reduction of uncontrolled emission percentage.
==PAGE:38==
14
Municipal Solid Waste
 Waste pre-processing and recycling are introduced for many facilities.
 Technology requirements and methods like best available control technol-
ogy (BACT) may be required.
Since the most common technology actually chosen for the recovery of wast en-
ergy content is the combustion process, new systems and processes are studied
to improve the air quality for MSW combustion (e.g.: optimization of com-
bustion chamber design, new materials development, gasication and thermal
treatment of waste).
In the Waste-to-Energy in Denmark report, the comparison between the emis-
sions of waste combustion facilities with coal, oil gas and natural gas power
plants is shown. It is highlighted that better results are obtained for the rst
system. Emissions of CO2, CH4, N2O, SO2 and NOx for dierent plants are
shown in Table2.3

It has to be focused that the carbon dioxide emission for the combustion of

2.2.2
Municipal Solid Waste Gasication
Gasication is a valiant alternative to combustion for the treatment of munic-
ipal solid waste, with the assisted gasication it is possible to create dierent
compositions of the products. Two main characteristics of the gasication pro-
cess are the possibility to produce fuels from unconventional feedstock and the
scalability of the system.
The similarity between gasication and combustion technology is that the pro-
cesses are based on chemical reactions of the fuel with an oxidant at high tem-
perature. The parameter that identify the two process is the air to fuel ratio,
==PAGE:39==
2.2 From Waste to Energy
15
usually described by the equivalence factor of Equation 2.1 which has inuence
on the temperature of the process.
φ =
h
air
fuel
i
stoichiometric
h
air
fuel
i
actual
(2.1)
In Figure 2.6 it is possible to see the temperature range for combustion and
gasication on the basis of the air supplied. The combustion process occurs
in excess of air while the gasication process takes place in sub-stoichiometric
conditions, usually less than 40% of the stoichiometric oxygen [21] is used. The
introduction of less air in the process leads to smaller gas production, meaning
that the total system dimension, in particular the cleaning system, can be re-
duced compared to combustion systems.
Besides it, the reduction of the cleaning gas system dimensions is obtained by
using gasication process, it is possible to keep some of the impurities in solid
phase and simplify the removal of them. Other products like sulfur and chlorine
are released under gaseous form and the removal of them is more dicult. Since
the composition of MSW is strongly variable it is necessary to develop a pol-
lution control system based on the waste used in the particular situation. The
University of California, Riverside, published in 2009 a report about gasication
emissions. Even though each plant under analysis had dierent characteristics,
all the systems give the following emission: particulate matter (PM), hydro-
gen chlorine (HCl), nitrous oxides (NOx), sulfur oxides (SOx), mercury (Hg)
dioxins and furans [21]. The cleaning system and the necessity to decompose
the tar, a mix of liquid and inorganic hydrocarbons, produced by gasication
process are the main limits to gasication technology spread.
The main combustion products are dioxins, particulates, chlorine, sulfur and
products of incomplete combustion.
Furthermore, the production of nitrous
oxides has to be considered because the combustion occurs at a temperature at
which thermal NOx can be formed. The reducing environment of the gasication
is favourable for the formation of N2 and NH3 rather than NOx. Moreover,
the gasication takes place at a lower temperature compared to thermal NOx
temperature of formation. One of the advantages of the gasication process
is that it makes possible to produce electricity as well as fuel or chemicals. In
combustion technologies all the fuel is oxidised to CO2 and H2O with additional
production of heat, whereas the gasication process is only a partial oxidation
during which intermediates like CO, H2 and hydrocarbons are formed. The
gasication process needs a deeper study of reactions occurring, but it allows
to obtain desired synthesis gas composition with minimal energy input into the
gasier and high fuel conversion level is reached.

2.3
Summary
The European Commission establishes a hierarchy for MSW management. The
recovery of energy from waste is set as the second to last options before landll-
ing. Waste to energy technology has been long based on combustion, actually
other ecient alternatives solutions like gasication have been introduced to
the market. Due to the strong variability of MSW the system developed for the
production of energy from waste need to be realized for the particular situation
under investigation.
==PAGE:41==
Chapter 3
Technology Overview
The technologies employed for the development of the plat under investigation
in this study are briey introduced in this chapter.
The gasication process is chosen for the production of syngas from municipal
solid waste. A solid oxide fuel cell plant is preferred as top cycle and a steam
cycle as bottoming. For the steam condensation after the steam turbine, it is
used a heat exchanger connected to a district heating network. Finally ue gas
energy content after the economizer is used in an absorption plant.
The integration of gasication process and solid oxide fuel cells with gas turbine
is extensively studied. The literature about SOFCs integration with Stirling
engine and steam cycle is not much.
3.1
Gasication
The gasication process is dened as the partial oxidation of a solid carbona-
ceous material like coal, biomass, oil or municipal solid waste. In this process
the fuel is broken down into carbon monoxide and hydrogen. Furthermore, the
production of carbon dioxide and hydrocarbon molecules like methane takes
place.
The main dierence between the products obtained by the combustion process
(ue gas) and those derived from the gasication process is in the heating value.
The synthesis gas produced by gasication, usually known by the name of syn-
gas, is a mixture of variable elements. The variability is basically due to fuel
and oxidant used for the process. Nevertheless, some components, like H2, CO,
==PAGE:42==
18
Technology Overview
CO2 and CH4, can be always found. Some of these components (e.g. H2 and
CH4) ensure an heating value such that the syngas can be burn. In particular,
for municipal solid waste it is possible to see that the lower heating value (LHV)
of the syngas strongly depends on the oxidant in use; it is usually included in
the range between 4 and 10 MJ/m3
N [23], higher values are achieved by using
oxygen as oxidant.
The gas obtained by the gasication process can also be used to produce energy
with modern technologies like SOFC (Solid Oxide Fuel Cell).
The oxidant used in the process strongly inuences the ratio between hydro-
gen and carbon monoxide in the syngas. On the base of the oxidant used for
gasication, it is possible to do the following classication [24]:
 partial oxidation with air,
 partial oxidation with oxygen,
 gasication with steam.
The use of oxygen or air as oxidant allows to have an exothermic gasication
process: heat is produced during the process and the enthalpy content of the
products is lower than that of the reactants. The gasication with steam is a
strongly endothermic process, due to this reason it is necessary to provide heat
to let the reactions occur.
Besides the three methods of gasication just seen, it is also possible to gasify
carbonaceous solid materials in an inert ambient such as N2. These conditions
require to supply heat to start the process.
The gasication process usually takes place between 500 and 1500  [25], never-
theless, the extraction temperature is strongly connected to the characteristics
of the feedstock and to the reactor in use.
The gasication process can be
divided in four steps [26]:
 Drying,
 Pyrolysis,
 Oxidation,
 Reduction.
Figure 3.1 shows a block diagram of the gasication process.
The drying process is necessary to remove the moisture content from the fuel.
This removal of moisture is of paramount importance when the fuel in use is
==PAGE:43==
3.1 Gasication
19
Figure 3.1: Gasication Process. Inspired to [26].
biomass or municipal solid waste, although the moisture content in this is lower
than in the biomass.
3.1.1
Drying Process
In general the drying process occurs between 110 and 140  [27] and it is con-
trolled by the Equation 3.1 [26]
˙Q = ˙mfuel(1 −MOI)cp,d(Td,out −Td, in)
+ ˙mfuelMOIcp,d(hsteamTd,out −hwaterTd,in)
(3.1)
where ˙Q is the heat needed to do the drying process, ˙mfuel is the ow of wet
fuel, MOI is the moisture percentage in the fuel, h is the enthalpy and the
subscript d indicates dried fuel.
The following draw schematically shows the conguration of the components in
the Two Stage Viking Gasier at the Technical University of Denmark. Gasier
data used in the development of the model are taken from Viking Gasier. It
==PAGE:44==
20
Technology Overview
was proven that the syngas produced by the gasier can be directly introduce
into the SOFC stacks [28].
Figure 3.2: Two Stage Viking Gasier. Inspired to [26].
3.1.2
Pyrolysis
Pyrolysis, known also as devolatilization or thermal decomposition, takes place
in the temperature range between 250 and 700 . This thermo-chemical process
is based on the reduction of chemical bonds followed by formation of simpler
and lighter molecules. Thanks to this process the solid fuel fed is converted into
gases, char, tar and ashes.
The process is governed by the reaction shown in Equation 3.2
4CnHm →mCH4 + (4n + m)C
(3.2)
At the end of the pyrolysis process it is possible to nd permanent gases, some
of them are CO, CO2, H2, CH4 and H2O. Furthermore, hydrocarbons, char
and tar are present at the end of the process.
Tar is a product of the pyrolysis, it is characterized by a dark color and it is
composed by long-chain and condensable organic compounds with high molec-
ular weight.
==PAGE:45==
3.1 Gasication
21
Char is a solid product of the pyrolysis developed after the production of light
gases and tars. High carbon content is measured in the char.
3.1.3
Oxidation
After the pyrolysis the char reacts with the oxygen present in the gasication
atmosphere and the oxidation takes place.
This process is characterized by
exothermic reactions, these reactions have to produce the heat essential for the
gasication. The whole process, including all the reactions taking place during
it, could be self-sustained. In general the oxidation occurs in scarcity of oxygen
and the most used approximation provides for oxidizing only carbonaceous ma-
terials. The main reactions in the oxidation process are shown in 3.3, 3.4, 3.5
and 3.6.
Table 3.1: Oxidation reactions with related enthalpy dierence [26] [29] [23].

The products of the oxidation step are two: heat releases from exothermic
reactions and a mixture of CO, CO2 and water vapour.
In excess of oxygen the reaction 3.4 occurs faster and the syngas temperature
would reach higher values. Since the gasication process consists of exothermic
and, especially, endothermic reactions, a higher temperature of the produced
gas will be helpful.
3.1.4
Reduction
Reduction is the last step of the gasication process and involves both the
pyrolysis and the oxidation products. Final syngas is the product of the reaction
between char and gaseous mixture. Table 3.2 shows the chemical endothermic
and exothermic reactions that characterize the reduction process and respective
enthalpy.

synthesis gas.

Gasication advantages
The trends of last years shows an increase in the energy cost. From 1983 to 2003
the oil price was in the range between 20 and 30 US$/bbl, while in the three
==PAGE:47==

years after 2003 the price was almost between 55 and 70 US$/bbl. A similar

years before 2003 was about 5-6 US$/MMBtu, in 2005 it was constant at 10
US$/MMBtu with a peak at the end of the year reaching 15 US$/MMBtu [24].
The syngas produced from gasication can be employed not only for power
production, but in many dierent elds like chemical sector (organic chemicals
production), production of hydrogen (the syngas obtained must be further pro-
cessed to reach high hydrogen purity), transportation (production of ethanol,
methanol, gasoline and diesel synthetic fuels) and it can replace the natural

3.2 Solid Oxide Fuel Cell (SOFC)
25
3.2
Solid Oxide Fuel Cell (SOFC)
The discover of the operating principle of fuel cells has to be attributed to
William Grove, in 1839 he reversed the water electrolysis to produce electricity
from hydrogen and oxygen. Fuel cells can convert a gaseous fuel in electric en-
ergy through electrochemical reactions. During the process some heat is released
and it can be used for other purposes. There is an anity between fuel cells and
batteries. The main dierence between the two mentioned technologies is that
the latter needs to be recharged. Fuel cells work thanks to the continuous ows
of fuel and oxidant supplied; when their supply is stopped no more electricity,
and consequently heat are produced.
3.2.1
Operating Principle of Solid Oxide Fuel Cells
At the beginning the only fuel used in the fuel cells was hydrogen, afterwards
would be discovered that others fuels were suitable for this technology. Since the
energy and the cost of H2 production are really high, the possibility to use other
fuels to feed the cells played an essential role in the development and propagation
of this technology. The exibility in using dierent fuels (natural gas, methanol,
ethanol, Di-Methyl Ether and syngas) is one of the main advantages of the fuel
cells.
Actually there are many types of fuel cells and the main classication is based
on the type of the electrolyte
 Alcaline Fuel Cell (AFC),
 Proton Exchange Membrane Fuel Cell (PEMFC),
 Direct Methanol Fuel Cell (DMFC),
 Phosphoric Acid Fuel Cell (PAFC),
 Molten Carbonate Fuel Cell (MCFC),
 Solid Oxide Fuel Cell (SOFC).
It is possible to make one more distinction between low and high operating
temperature fuel cells.
AFC, PEMFC and DMFC belong to the rst group
and their operating temperature is around 90 . PAFC, MCFC and SOFC are
high temperature cells.
Figure 3.5 schematically represents the ows of fuel
and oxidant in the dierent types of cells and the relative range of operating
temperatures.
==PAGE:50==
26
Technology Overview
Figure 3.5: Fuell Cell Types. Inspired to [31].
The fuel considered in Figure 3.5 is pure hydrogen, except for DMFC in which
the fuel is methanol. The only product obtained from electrochemical reactions
occurring is hot water that could be used for district heating.
The essential components of a fuel cell are four:
 Anode: it is the positive electrode through which the fuel is inserted into
the cell.
 Electrolyte: it is necessary to separate the two electrodes and to allow the
ow of produced ions.
 Cathode: it is the negative electrode through which the oxidant is inserted
into the cell.
 External electric circuit: it is necessary to guarantee the electrons ow.
==PAGE:51==
3.2 Solid Oxide Fuel Cell (SOFC)
27
The fuel (e.g: hydrogen, natural gas or syngas) passes through the anode and
it is ionized letting an electron on the anode surface. The latter is a conductor
and keeps the electrons close to the electrolyte. At the same time a stockpile of
positive charges would occur in the electrolyte due to the electrostatic strength
so that an electric potential will rise. After a while the equilibrium is reached:
from one side the reaction would continue to provide electron, from the other
side the electric potential would drive the positive ions towards the electrode
and would neutralize them. Equilibrium conditions dene the ideal equilibrium
potential for the electrode. At the cathode side we have a similar situation, the
only dierence is that in this case the electrode cedes electrons.
All the previous observations refer to the single separated electrodes. Connect-
ing them with a conductor it will be possible to let the electrons circulate on
the external electric circuit. The electricity can be supplied to an external load.
The electrodes, anode and cathode, should reduce the barriers for the reactions
by simplifying the diusion of reagent gases and the removal of products from
the reaction. They are usually made with porous media on which the catalyst
(like platinum and gold) is adsorbed. The area of the electrode is of paramount
importance to let the reactions occur. Porous electrodes made with metal pow-
ders and carbon compounds are suitable for this purpose, in this manner the
depth of the electrode is between 5 and 50 µm [32].
Operating principle of fuel cells is based on oxidation and reduction reactions
occurring respectively at anode and cathode side. Reactions taking place at
SOFC electrodes are shown in Table 3.4.
Table 3.4: Anode, cathode and global reactions for a fuel cell. Adapted from
[33].
Anode side
H2 + O2−→H2O + 2e2−+ heat
(3.16)

The ideal eciency of a cell, ηideal, can be dened as the ratio between the ideal

this is equal to the enthalpy dierence of the reaction.

Voltage Losses
When the circuit is closed, meaning that the current ow is not zero, real voltage
across the cell is substantially lower than the theoretical voltage. This decrease
in the voltage is due to losses that can be classied as:
 Ohmic Polarization (Eohm): this is caused by the resistance to the ion
ow in the electrolyte and by the resistance to the electron ow in the
electrode.
 Concentration Polarization (Econc): this loss arises only when the electri-
cal current is high. It is due to the diculty of reagents to spread inside
==PAGE:55==
3.2 Solid Oxide Fuel Cell (SOFC)
31
the electrolyte and/or to the diculty of products to let space for new
reagents with a velocity that ensures the electrical current level desired.
These phenomena give rise to a decrease in voltage.
 Activation Polarization (Eact): it is due to chemical reactions that occur
at the electrodes, these reactions can cause chemical variations on the
electrodes surface and adsorption of gaseous reagents. It is also possible
that a certain voltage level is required to let the electrons move away from
the electrode where they were developed.
It is possible to write the real voltage of a fuel cell as in Equation 3.25
Ecell = Eopen −Eohm −Econc −Eact
(3.25)
Figure 3.7 shows the curves for each kind of loss.
The ideal cell voltage is
represented by an horizontal straight line, it is necessary to remove the voltage
losses from the ideal trend. The real curve obtained by the removal of each loss
is really dierent from the theoretical one, as it is possible to see in the graph
(e) of Figure 3.7.
Three main parameters inuence the operation of a cell:
1. Temperature
The Equation 3.24 can also be written by introducing enthalpy, H, and
entropy, S, in the following way
E = −∆H
nF + T∆S
nF
(3.26)
The enthalpy dierence, ∆H, is negative as well as the entropy dierence,
∆S, for the majority of the reactions occurring in the fuel cell. This means
that by increasing the temperature the voltage decreases. Nevertheless,
the temperature increase lets the activation losses decrease. The result of
the combined eect of these two results is that the cell eciency increases.
Some positive eects of the temperature increase are
 Reduction of ohmic polarization, it is due to the concurrent decrease
of the electrical conductivity in the conductors, and increase of ionic
conductivity in the electrolyte.
==PAGE:56==
32
Technology Overview
Figure 3.7: Voltage Losses in fuel cell. Inspired to [35].
 Reduction of concentration polarization thanks to the improvement
of global transport phenomena (gases, reagents and products).
 Reduction of activation polarization caused by the increased velocity
of electrochemical reactions.
However, the high temperature provokes corrosion, problems of sintering
and recrystallization of catalysts and evaporation of the electrolyte.
2. Pressure
The adduction of gases in the cell can occur at atmospheric or at high
pressure.
Although the high pressure adduction allows to have higher
reversible potential, E, more transport phenomena and higher solubility
in the electrolyte, the high pressure adduction needs some precautions.
Any explosion and formation of explosive substance must to be avoided,
this is possible by using high sealing materials and more controls.
3. Fuel Composition
The increase of hydrogen content in the fuel gas and of oxygen content in
the oxidant gas does not always help to improve the eciency of the cell.
This is feasible by increasing the complexity of the cleaning fuel system
and of the ltration air system. Anyway, the impurities aect negatively
the eciency of the cell.
==PAGE:57==
3.2 Solid Oxide Fuel Cell (SOFC)
33
Reforming
Reforming is the process through which hydrogen is extracted from light hy-
drocarbons. Natural gases and methane are the most used gases for reforming
process. The chemical endothermic reaction that occurs in presence of natural
gas is
CH4 + H2O →3H2 + CO
(3.27)
It takes place at high temperature, between 700 and 900  [32], it is necessary
to provide heat with a burner to let the reaction happen.
The resulting gas is rich of hydrogen and contains also CO which creates prob-
lems in some kind of cells, PEM and PAFC are particularly intolerant to carbon
monoxide. This leads to the need of converting CO in CO2 by the reaction 3.28,
known as CO shift. The removal of carbon monoxide by cleaning up the gas is
essential.
CO + H2O →H2 + CO2
(3.28)
The resulting gas is ready to be sent to the SOFC. Thanks to CO shift reaction
the content of CO in the gas can be reduce to 0.5%, after the gas clean up the
amount of CO in the syngas can be lower than 10ppmv (parts per million by
volume).
SOFCs and MCFCs are not intolerant to CO and the eciency of the reforming
process can be reduced, this allows to have easier and cheaper process.
The reforming process takes place inside the reformer which can be either ex-
ternal and internal. Internal reforming enables to supply CH4, natural gas or
any kind of synthesis gas (like municipal solid waste or biomass syngas) directly
to the cell. Furthermore, Internal reforming allows to exploit all the heat de-
veloped by the cell and the burner removal is possible, this is feasible only in
the high temperature cells where the heat produced is enough to support the
process (SOFC and MCFC). One of the main problem occurring by providing
synthesis gases to the cells is due to the presence of impurities, in particular
sulfur is really dangerous and poisons the anode.
==PAGE:58==
34
Technology Overview
3.3
Integrated Gasication SOFC Plants
The possibility to supply SOFCs with synthesis gas has led to the integration of
gasication plants with SOFC plants. The syngas obtained by the gasication
process is fed to the SOFCs to produce electric power. Since the fuel utilization
factor of the cells is not 100% (usually values of fuel utilization factor up to 80%
can be achieved, in particular cases it is possible to nd also higher values) not
reacted fuel is found after the fuel cells, it can be introduced into a burner. An
oxidant is introduced into the burner to oxidise the fuel, the outgoing o-gases
can be used to fuel a Stirling engine, a gas turbine plant or a steam cycle plant.
3.3.1
Integrated SOFC and Stirling Engine Plant
The system investigated in [36] is a small-scale integrated gasication, solid
oxide fuel cell and Stirling engine. A draw of the plant in object is shown in
Figure 3.8.
Figure 3.8: Integrated gasication, solid oxide fuel cells and Stirling engine
plant. Inspired to [36].
The plant of Figure 3.8 has a net electric capacity is 120 kWe. The gasication
process is composed by two steps: in the rst one the feedstock is dried and
the pyrolysis takes place, in the second step the gasication with steam and air
occurs in a xed bed reactor. The gas cleaner is necessary to remove H2S from
syngas and its content would be lower than 1ppm, the operating temperature
==PAGE:59==
3.3 Integrated Gasication SOFC Plants
35
of this component is accept to be 250 .
The methanator is useful to increase the methane molar fraction in the syn-
gas from about 0.01 to approximately 0.05.
The syngas outgoing from the
methanator is characterized by higher molar fractions of N2 and CO2 while the
contents of hydrogen and carbon monoxide are decreased. This change in syn-
gas composition does not substantially aect the electrical production. Since
the reforming is strongly exothermic the temperature of the syngas going to the
anode pre-heater is high. This leads to a minor subtraction of heat from SOFCs
o-fuel and it is helpful to provide high temperature ow to the Stirling engine.
Furthermore, a high content of CH4 into the cells lets the internal reforming
occur and then less air to cool down the system is required, this brings about a
reduction of air blower workload.
SOFCs are supposed to work at 780  and normal pressure, the compressors
in the plant have to compensate the pressure drop along the way. The cathode
pre-heater (CP) works with a part of the o-air stream from the SOFC plant.
The other o-air going out from fuel cells is introduced into the burner as oxi-
dant. Since the Stirling engine works better with higher temperature, it is more
advisable to have high temperature ue gas from the burner. It is possible to
obtain this result by reducing the ow of colder o-air reaching the burner.
Finally, the bottoming cycle is a Stirling engine fed with combustion products
from the burner. The heat content after the Stirling engine is used to produce
hot water for domestic purpose. At the end the ue gas outgoing from the sys-
tem has to has enough high temperature to avoid corrosion problems, for the
plant studied in [36] this temperature is 95 .
Feedstock mass ow, number of stacks and fuel cells utilization factor are the
three parameters investigated in the paper [36]. It is possible to observe that
by increasing the ow rate the thermal eciency of the plant decreases. On
the other hand the eciency increases with the number of stacks, but it must
be remembered that the cost of the fuel cells is still really high. Regarding the
utilization factor it is possible to see a fast eciency decrease above a certain
value of this parameter. The value, in correspondence of which the rapid decline
starts, is a function of the number of stacks used.
The use of a Stirling engine as bottoming cycle is less complex than gas turbine
or steam cycle. Usually the use of a Stirling engine as bottom cycle of a SOFC
plant allows to reach high value of the heat-power ratio and small scale plants
can be realized (e.g: plants for shopping centres, hospitals and hotels). In [37]
and [36] the size of the plants studied is 120 kWel, this is suitable for decentral-
ized combined heat-power (CHP) plants. The eciency achieved is 42.4% while
the production price of electricity and the cost of hot water are 0.1204 $/kWh
and 0.0214 $/kWh respectively.
The use of combined gasication, SOFC and Stirling engine plants as decentral-
ized CHP plants is also described in [14]. It is explain how the introduction of
the methanator, in the fuel treatment line, increases the current temperature.
The anode pre-heater could be no longer necessary to reach the desired tem-
==PAGE:60==
36
Technology Overview
perature before the fuel cells. The plant investigated in [14], with methanator
and without anode pre-heater, reaches a thermal eciency 1% higher than that
obtained with anode pre-heater or anode pre-heater plus methanator congura-
tions.
By enlarging the number of stacks an increase in the electrical eciency up to
48%[14] can be observed, nevertheless it would be economically disadvantageous.
3.3.2
Integrated SOFC and Gas Turbine Plants
Integrated gasication, SOFC and gas turbine plants have been extensively stud-
ied in the past. For large scale plants (around 5 MWel) this solution allows to
obtain thermal eciency (LHV based) around 60% [38].
Bang-Møller and Rokni in [38] studied dierent solutions of integrated plants:
gasication plant integrated with a micro gas turbine, gasication plant inte-
grated with a solid oxide fuel cell plant and the integration of the gasication
plant, SOFCs and micro gas turbine all together. The eciency achieved with
this small scale plant is around 50%. The object of the study is a plant produc-
ing more or less 250 kWel
One of the most important parameters that inuence the eciency of this cat-
egory of plants is the pressure ratio. For the conguration with gasier, SOFCs
and MGT the maximum eciency is reached with a pressure ratio of about 2.5
[38], by increasing the ratio the eciency decreases. It is necessary to remem-
ber that the value of the pressure ratio that gives the maximum eciency is
strongly connected with the conguration of the plant. It is important to note
that by combining SOFCs and gas turbine technologies the fuel cells must work
in pressurized conditions. Maintaining the cells at a pressure rather high is not
a minor issue, it is energetically and economically expensive. Regarding the
pressurization of SOFCs, Lim et al. [39] designed an anode-supported planar
SOFC stack for a 5 kW SOFC-GT plant at the Korea Institute of Energy Re-
search. One of the focus of the study was the operation at 3.5 atm (absolute).
By using liqueed natural gas (LNG) pre-reformed as fuel in the solution with
micro gas turbine the SOFC stack produces 5.1 kW at 3.5 atm (absolute). 4.7
kW are obtained at atmospheric pressure with pre-reformed gas while 8.1 kW
are reached at atmospheric pressure using hydrogen. The experimental results
shown as the anode-supported planar SOFC stack operates well in the hybrid
system studied.
A signicant role is performed by temperatures:
turbine inlet temperature
(TIT), turbine outlet temperature (TOT), air compressor outlet temperature
(COT) and in particular SOFCs operating temperature. Actually SOFCs op-
erating temperature is included in the range between 750 and 1000 , it is
basically related to the used electrolyte [28]. New studies introduce the possi-
bility to reduce the operating temperature of the SOFC to 650 , this reduction
==PAGE:61==
3.3 Integrated Gasication SOFC Plants
37
makes the integration of this kind of fuel cells with steam cycle more suitable
than that with a gas turbine [28].
Furthermore, one more aspect should be
considered by integrating SOFC with GT, the start up time of SOFC plant is
much longer than that of GT plant. In case of SOFC-GT integration lowers the
SOFCs operating temperature would decrease the cost of the materials, but, at
the same time, the performances of the plant would change. The total electrical
eciency of the three plants designed in [38] will decrease with the reduction of
SOFCs operating temperature.
The rst two system of SOFC-GT integration were developed by Siemens West-
inghouse to Southern Californian Edison (220 kWel) in May 2000 and by Ontario
Hydro, Canada (250 kWel and 145 kWth).
Haseli et al. [40] show that the maximum eciency is achieved for a compression
ratio equal to 4 like in a conventional gas turbine plant, but in the integrated
plant the eciency is much higher, as shown in Figure 3.9. In particular, it is
demonstrated that the eciency of a SOFC-GT integrated plant is about 27.8%
higher than conventional GT plant. The irreversibility of the plant increases
by increasing the pressure ratio, it is mainly due to the more workload of the
compressor.
One more study on integrated SOFC-GT plant is realised by Calise et al. in [41].
The eciency of 60% is reached in a plant of 1.5 MW without heat loss recovery,
by recovering the loss an eciency value of about 70% is achieved. This study
shows as it is possible to increase the global eciency of the plant design under
study by increasing the operating temperature of the SOFCs. However, this
increase leads higher costs, in particular for compressor and turbine.
3.3.3
Integrate SOFC and Steam Cycle Plants
The trend in the SOFCs research is to reduce the operating temperature to
allow the use of cheaper materials and decrease the costs. This reduction in
SOFCs operating temperature make the integration between SOFC and steam
cycle more attractive than the integration with a gas turbine [28].
In the investigation given in [28] is studied an integrated gasication, SOFC and
steam cycle plant; two congurations, with and without the hybrid recuperator,
are analysed. The steam cycle is modelled with a simple single pressure level.
The fuel used in the study has a lower heating value equal to 18280 kJ/kg,
specic heat of 1.35 kJ/(kgK) and a moisture content of 33.2 %. The analysis
underlines as the increase in live steam pressure has two consequences: the
eciency decrease of the top cycle and the increase of the burner temperature.
This causes higher steam temperature and the enthalpy drop over the steam
turbine increases, a better eciency of the bottoming cycle is achieved. The
increase of live steam pressure, in the hybrid recuperator design, brings about
==PAGE:62==
38
Technology Overview
Figure 3.9: Comparison of thermal eciency at dierent compression ratio
between integrated SOFC-GT and simple GT plants. Taken from
[40].
the decrease of heat recovery steam generator eciency, the heat absorbed by
the steam cycle is reduced. The eciency of the plat in the design with hybrid
recuperator achieve the value of 62% under particular conditions. Figure 3.10
and in Figure 3.11 show the heat absorbed by the steam plant as a function
of live steam pressure, for plant design without and with hybrid recuperator
respectively.
The fuel used in [28] is woodchips. Few other studies of an integrated SOFC-
steam cycle plant were carried out, two of them are [42] and [43] where the fuel
utilized is natural gas (NG). In both cases it is possible to reach an eciency
around 70%, much higher than the one achievable with biomass. These high
values are achieved with the use of hybrid recuperator.
The importance of
removing sulfur becomes fundamental by using natural gas as fuel. Moreover
the prereformer has to be considered into the plant design.
Dierent solutions considering adiabatic steam reformer (ASR) and catalytic
partial oxidation (CPO) reactors are investigated in [42]. ASR reactor needs
superheated steam during the start up while CPO requires additional air to pre-

3.4
District Heating
District heating is a system that supplies the heat produced in a centralized
location by an underground isolated piping network. The network can provide
hot water for heating, hot tap water and also steam for industrial process. The
heat is usually produced by a CHP plant using fossil fuels, natural gas, biomass
or municipal solid waste for the production of electricity.
In country using
nuclear power it is possible to recover heat from nuclear plant, geothermal and
solar sources can also be used.
The outlet temperature of district heating is usually around 90  while the
inlet temperature is generally in the range between 30 and 60 . Nevertheless,

States and some networks are also in north China and in Japan. Reference is
made to Danish experience in this little introduction. The use of district heat-

energy eciency level.
The rst Danish CHP was realised in 1903. From waste incineration was pos-
sible to obtain electrical energy and heat. In the following decades was built a
district heating system based on the use of heat released from waste electricity
production. The district heating achievement was immediately demonstrated,
in fact, this new technology was able to supply around 4% of Danish heat. In
1970 the heat produced by district heating satised around 30% of home heat
request in Denmark. Nowadays about 63% of all houses in the country is served
by district heating for either heating and hot tap water. In 2013 the production
of Danish district heating was equal to 134 PJ (petajoule) and all the produc-
tion was derived from CHP, this allows to save about 30% of fuel compared with
separate generation of heat and power [45]. Figure 3.12 shows a diagram of dis-

in Denmark. Figure 3.13 shows the district heating network of Copenhagen.

excluded from heat taxes charged only on heat produced by fossil fuels. More-

around 40-50 years. Actually district heating is the most feasible solution in

3.5 Absorption Plant
43
3.5
Absorption Plant
The main characteristic that distinguishes absorption chillers from compression
chillers is the kind of energy they use. Compression chillers are alimented by
mechanical energy usually obtained by electrical engines, meaning that electric
energy must be consumed to let them work. The production of this energy can
occur in dierent ways. The dierent conditions between low and high pressure
in an absorption chiller are obtained thanks to an absorbent substance and the
energy used is thermal energy. In this kind of chillers the heat, transferred from
a high temperature source, is used to produce cooling power. The absorption
plant is essentially composed by four main components
 Desorber or Generator,
 Condenser,
 Evaporator,
 Absorber.
Figure 3.14 shows a block diagram of an absorption chiller. Two-stages chillers
are also available on the market, they can achieve better performance, but the
hot temperature source for these machines must be much higher than that in
one-stage plant.
Dierent sources of heat can be used in the absorption chiller: hot water, super-
heated water, vapours, ue gas or fuel oil. The thermodynamic properties of a
binary mixture are used in the absorption plant, two kinds of absorption plant
cycle are actually on the market:
 H2O−NH3 cycle in which the refrigerant is NH3 and the solvent is H2O.
 H2O −LiBr cycle where H2O is the refrigerant and LiBr is the solvent,
it is a non-toxic crystalline salt with high anity for water and with high
boiling point.
Plants based on H2O −NH3 cycle require more components than plant devel-
oped for the H2O−LiBr cycle. One of the advantages of the H2O−NH3 chillers
is that they do not need to operate under strict low pressure conditions, this
is an economic saving. In the H2O −LiBr plants, water is used as refrigerant
uid; since the crystallization temperature of water, at atmospheric pressure, is
==PAGE:68==
44
Technology Overview
Figure 3.14: Block diagram of the operating mechanism of an absorption
chiller.
0 , this system cannot be used under such temperature. This is not a strict
limit because in air conditioning system the temperature is always higher than
0 , for this reason the H2O −LiBr chillers are abundantly used in this eld.
The water triple point is at 0.01  and 661 Pa, it is possible to conclude that
at a very low pressure the use of H2O −LiBr absorption plants could be taken
into account for temperatures under 0 .
3.5.1
Operating Principle of a H2O−LiBr Absorption Chiller
The plant used in the present study is a single stage H2O −LiBr absorption
plant. In this paragraph are shown the functions of each component of the plant.
Desorber: the high temperature source heats the diluted solution of water and
lithium bromide. The water boiling occurs and the concentrate solution of LiBr
is separated from the steam that will be used as refrigerant. The concentrated
solution of LiBr is then sent to a valve to lower the pressure before going to
the absorber.
Condenser: the refrigerant vapour generated into the reformer arrives to the
condenser where it condensates on the surface of the cooling coils. The con-
densing heat is removed from coolant water and then drained through a cooling
==PAGE:69==
3.5 Absorption Plant
45
tower. The condensate uid has to pass through a trolling valve to lower its
pressure before arriving into the evaporator.
Evporator: the pressure in the evaporator, as well as pressure at the absorber,
is much lower than the pressure at the desorber and in the condenser. This low
pressure allows the boiling of the refrigerant liquid into the evaporator at low
temperature. The latent heat of evaporation is supplied by a low temperature
external uid that will be cooled by the evaporator. The refrigerant steam ob-
tained ows into the absorber.
Absorber: the low pressure, roughly 8 mbar, into the absorber is due to the
chemical anity between the concentrated solution of LiBr and the refrigeration
vapour formed into the evaporator. This vapour is absorbed by the concentrated
solution of LiBr. The cooling water aims to remove the heat of dilution. The
absorber has the function of bringing the refrigerant vapour back to the liquid
phase, in a compressor chiller this is the role of the compressor.
The process just described allows to use a heat source at a relatively low temper-
ature, usually between 70 and 95  [46], to obtain refrigerated water available,
for example, for civil or industrial applications. The process is convenient when
the wasted heat can be recovered, for instance, in combined heat and power
plants. The use of o-gas from a CHP to fuel the desorber of an absorption
plant enable the trigeneration production like in the present study.
Absorption Plant Eciency
The parameter that allows to calculate the performance of an absorption plant
is called coecient of performance (COP). It is given by the ration between the
cooling power produced at the evaporator and the thermal power introduced
into the generator.
COP = Cooling
Power
at
the
Evaporator[kW]
Thermal
Power
at
the
Generator[kW]
(3.29)
The COP for H2O −LiBr chillers usually varies between 0.7 and 1.2 [47], it is
strongly connected to the following parameters:
 Possibility of concentrating again the solution,
 Temperature available at the desorber,
 Refrigerant uid temperature at the evaporator,
==PAGE:70==
46
Technology Overview
 Coolant uid temperature at the condenser.
The COP of an absorption machine cannot be compared with the COP of a
compression chiller. Even if the operating temperatures are the same the en-
ergy required for the operation is of a dierent type.
Dierent refrigeration cycles are actually under study, as investigated in [48].
The cooling power produced by the absorption plant can be used in dierent
ways, one of them could be district cooling. This technology has similar oper-
ating principle of district heating. The chilled uid produced in a centralized
plant is sent to buildings that need cooling power through a piping network.
This kind of system is more advisable for hot countries, large oce buildings or
industrial cooling.
3.6
Summary
In this chapter are analysed dierent technologies for the production of electric-
ity, heating and cooling. The possibility of their integration is evaluated in the
next chapter integrated. The ue gas energy of a power plant for electricity pro-
duction can be partially recovered, for example for the production of hot water
for domestic purpose. The additional use of an absorption chiller to produce
cooling power allows to increase the quantity of energy recovered.
The global eciency of a trigeneration plant is higher than that of a plant for
electricity production, a bigger percentage of the caloric value of the fuel is
used.
The COP of absorption machines achieves by using them in the trigeneration
plants is lower than that of a chiller itself. The main advantage is in the use of
heat coming from electrical power generation that otherwise would be lost.
==PAGE:71==
Chapter 4
Software and Methods
The thermodynamic analysis carried out in this study is realized with the use
of DNA (Dynamic Network Analysis) simulation tools. This software is an on-
going development process in the Thermal Energy Section of the Mechanical
Department at Technical University of Denmark.
Dierent designs of trigeneration plants are developed and thermodynamically
analysed. The DNA results are compared and the most favourable options were
highlighted. The optimization, in terms of thermal eciency, is carried out for
each plant developed. Finally, the economic aspect is taken into consideration
and the payback time of the investment is calculated for dierent solutions.
Evaluations are done by employing the software Microsoft Excel©.
4.1
Dynamic Network Analysis (DNA)
DNA software arises from a Master's Thesis project [49] and the code of the
program is written in FORTRAN. The software is endlessly under development
and thanks to this continual work general applications are possible, achieving
unique features. DNA can also be used to supplement other simulation pro-
grams.
Dynamic Network Analysis contains an ample component library including heat
exchangers, turbo machinery, engines, generators, burners, gasiers, separators
and mixers, valves, dryers, fuel cells and many other components and utility
components.
==PAGE:72==
48
Software and Methods
The system developments with DNA are realized connecting the dierent com-
ponents by nodes and adding operating conditions for the complete system. This
characteristic makes the software a component-based simulation tools. After
having connected all the nodes and having integrated the code with additional
conditions, the physical model is transformed in a set of mathematical equation
that are solved numerically. The set of equations under investigation includes
the mass and energy conservation for all components and nodes. Furthermore,
the relations for thermodynamic properties of ows compounds are included in
DNA. Heat losses and exchanges between the components of the system are
evaluated in the total energy and mass balance. The software is provided with
constitutive equations representing the physical properties of the component
studied [50].
DNA is also equipped with a library of predened medium that can be used in
the system, nevertheless, any kind of solid, liquid and gaseous mixture can be
manually introduced. A change in the composition of the medium is expected
after some components (e.g: gasier, gas cleaner, methanator, etc.) and the
program automatically calculates this variation and gives back the composition
as output [28].
A block diagram of the process under study is illustrated in Figure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: General block diagram of the plant studied.
Municipal solid waste (MSW) reacts with air and syngas and ash are produced.
Hydrogen sulde has to be removed before fuelling the synthesis gas into the
SOFCs anode. Thermochemical reactions occurring into SOFC plant produce
electric power.
Not reacted fuel and air are then combusted into a burner.
==PAGE:73==
4.1 Dynamic Network Analysis (DNA)
49
The heat contained in ue gas coming out of the burner is used in a heat
recovery steam generator (HRSG). Steam produced is used in a simple single
pressure level steam cycle. The mechanical power produced by the cycle has to
be converted into electric power. The energy recovered after the steam turbine
is available for district heating. The remaining thermal content in the ue gas
is used with an absorption chiller.
In this chapter are briey introduced the equations implemented in DNA for
some of the components of the plant, special attention is reserved to Gasier
and Solid Oxide Fuel Cell models.
4.1.1
Gasier Model
A diagram of the gasier is shown in Figure 4.2. The gasier model is based
on the Viking Gasier developed by the Biomass Gasication Group at the
Technical University of Denmark, Roskilde [51]. The Viking gasier plant is a
75
kWth x bed biomass gasier. The rst reactor is responsible for the drying
and the pyrolysis process of the fuel, after that reactor the pyrolytic gas, at the
temperature of 600 , reaches an autothermal downdraft gasication reactor.
The energy of exhausted gases after the gasier is recovered for the drying and
pyrolysis process [36].
Dried fuel is introduced into the gasier as well as steam and oxidant, syngas
and ash come out from the component. Heat losses are also considered in the
component model.
The model of the gasier is based on a Gibbs reactor, under this hypothesis the
equilibrium is reached when the Gibbs free energy arrives at its minimum. These
features allow to calculate the syngas composition at a specied temperature
and pressure without considering the reaction pathway [52]. The method used
for syngas composition calculation is synthetically illustrated here referring to
[28].

4.1.2
Solid Oxide Fuel Cell Model
The component diagram of SOFC is illustrated in Figure 4.3. Fuel and air are
introduced respectively at the anode side and at the cathode side, while o-
fuel and o-air come out respectively from anode and cathode. Heat losses and
electric power are given as output.
Figure 4.3: Solid oxide fuel cell component diagram.
The DNA SOFC model employed in this investigation requires to set some oper-
ating parameters of the cell. The parameters specied are fuel utilization factor,
operating temperature, anode pressure drop, cathode pressure drop, number of
cells in a stack and the number of stacks used. In particular, the analysis of the
plants given in this study will show what happen by changing fuel utilization
factor, operating temperature and the number of stacks.
The inlet temperature at the cathode side and, in some conguration, even the
inlet temperature at the anode side are set in this study. The characteristics of
fuel entering SOFC plant (mass ow, temperature, pressure and composition)
are the results of the fuel path before arriving to the stacks. The mass ow of
air reaching the cathode is automatically calculated by the program based on
==PAGE:77==
4.1 Dynamic Network Analysis (DNA)
53
the syngas features at the anode side.
DNA outputs are electrical power produced by the cells and o-gas composi-
tions. The gas composition calculation is based on chemical equilibrium. The
electric power generated is evaluated through a detailed electrochemical model
that makes use of empirical data.
The model is validated between 650 and
800  where experimental data are available. In this study a wider range of
operating temperature is simulated, from 650 to 900 . Any value out of the
validated range is used for the plant optimization.
The SOFC model implemented in DNA and considered in this study was de-
veloped in [38] and it is explained here. This model is based on planar cells
developed by Risø and Topsøe Fuel Cell (topsoefuelcell.com). The SOFC type
under study is anode supported, with Ni/YSZ1 anode, a YSZ electrolyte and
an LSM2/YSZ cathode [53]. The validation of the model against experimen-
tal data was done in the operating temperature range between 650 and 800 .
The model takes into account the distinction between electrochemical modeling,
calculation of cell irreversibility (cell voltage eciency) and the species compo-
sitions at the outlet.
The operational cell voltage is given by the following equation

Other Components Model
Other components used in the system are dryer, heat exchangers, desulfurizer,
compressors, burner, steam turbine, pumps, deaerator, desorber, condenser, ab-
sorber, valves and some plants are designed with a methanator. Some of them
are of particular interest.
Dierent kinds of dryer are available on DNA, the one used in the system un-
der investigation required to specify the pressure drop inside the component for
==PAGE:82==

Software and Methods
both solid and steam currents.
The gas cleaner under investigation allows to remove the desired amount of any
compound, hydrogen sulphide in this case.
Two assumptions are done in the present investigation; the rst one is that the
heat exchangers model neglects the heat losses and the second one supposes
that all the energy from one side is transferred to the other side. In the soft-
ware are implemented dierent kind of heat exchangers, on the basis of the heat
exchanger topology either LMDT (Logarithmic Mean Temperature Dierence)
and ε −NTU (Eectiveness-Number of Transferred Unit) methods are imple-
mented [28].
The power consumption of compressors is evaluated on the base of isentropic
and mechanical eciency, they are dened in Equation 4.24 and Equation 4.25

The methanator, used in some of the solutions, increases the methane content
in the syngas. The metanation reaction occurring in this component is
CO + 3H2 →CH4 + H2O
this is an exothermic reaction, but it is not the only one taking place in the
component. Other minor reactions occur depending on the fuel used. Also for
this component a simple Gibbs reactor is implemented, the minimum of the
Gibbs free energy is reached when the chemical equilibrium is achieved. The
species considered at the outlet are H2, N2, CO, CO2, H2O, CH4 and Ar.
The DNA burner model used in the system needs only to set the pressure ratio
between the inlet and outlet pressures. The ue gas composition is automatically
calculated by the program.
